English</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">&#160;•&#160;<a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_United_States" title="Languages of the United States">Spoken <p><b>Ohio</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;h&#39; in &#39;hi&#39;">h</span><span title="/aɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;tide&#39;">aɪ</span><span title="/./: syllable break">.</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span>&#32;<span class="noprint"><span class="ext-phonos"><span data-nosnippet="" id="ooui-php-1" class="ext-phonos-PhonosButton noexcerpt ext-phonos-PhonosButton-emptylabel oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-buttonElement oo-ui-buttonElement-frameless oo-ui-iconElement oo-ui-buttonWidget" data-ooui="{&quot;_&quot;:&quot;mw.Phonos.PhonosButton&quot;,&quot;href&quot;:&quot;\/\/upload.wikimedia.org\/wikipedia\/commons\/transcoded\/e\/e4\/En-us-Ohio.ogg\/En-us-Ohio.ogg.mp3&quot;,&quot;rel&quot;:[&quot;nofollow&quot;],&quot;framed&quot;:false,&quot;icon&quot;:&quot;volumeUp&quot;,&quot;data&quot;:{&quot;ipa&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;text&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;lang&quot;:&quot;en&quot;,&quot;wikibase&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;file&quot;:&quot;En-us-Ohio.ogg&quot;},&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;ext-phonos-PhonosButton&quot;,&quot;noexcerpt&quot;,&quot;ext-phonos-PhonosButton-emptylabel&quot;]}"><a role="button" tabindex="0" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/e4/En-us-Ohio.ogg/En-us-Ohio.ogg.mp3" rel="nofollow" aria-label="Play audio" title="Play audio" class="oo-ui-buttonElement-button"><span class="oo-ui-iconElement-icon oo-ui-icon-volumeUp"></span><span class="oo-ui-labelElement-label"></span><span class="oo-ui-indicatorElement-indicator oo-ui-indicatorElement-noIndicator"></span></a></span><sup class="ext-phonos-attribution noexcerpt navigation-not-searchable"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Ohio.ogg" title="File:En-us-Ohio.ogg">ⓘ</a></sup></span></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">oh-<span style="font-size:90%">HY</span>-oh</i></a>)<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">state</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwestern</a> region of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. Ohio borders <a href="/wiki/Lake_Erie" title="Lake Erie">Lake Erie</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> to the east, <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a> to the southeast, <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a> to the southwest, <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> to the west, and <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a> to the northwest. Of the 50 <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_and_territories_of_the_United_States" title="List of states and territories of the United States">U.S. states</a>, it is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_area" title="List of U.S. states and territories by area">34th-largest by area</a>. With a population of nearly 11.8 million, Ohio is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_population" title="List of U.S. states and territories by population">seventh-most populous</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_population_density" class="mw-redirect" title="List of U.S. states and territories by population density">tenth-most densely populated state</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/List_of_capitals_in_the_United_States" title="List of capitals in the United States">capital</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Ohio" class="mw-redirect" title="List of cities in Ohio">most populous city</a> is <a href="/wiki/Columbus,_Ohio" title="Columbus, Ohio">Columbus</a>, with other large <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_statistical_area" title="Metropolitan statistical area">population centers</a> including <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati" title="Cincinnati">Cincinnati</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dayton,_Ohio" title="Dayton, Ohio">Dayton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Akron,_Ohio" title="Akron, Ohio">Akron</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Toledo,_Ohio" title="Toledo, Ohio">Toledo</a>. Ohio is nicknamed the "Buckeye State" after its <a href="/wiki/Aesculus_glabra" title="Aesculus glabra">Ohio buckeye trees</a>, and Ohioans are also known as "Buckeyes".<sup id="cite_ref-DNR_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DNR-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/State_flag_of_Ohio" class="mw-redirect" title="State flag of Ohio">Its flag</a> is the only <a href="/wiki/List_of_non-rectangular_flags" title="List of non-rectangular flags">non-rectangular flag</a> of all U.S. states.
</p><p>Ohio derives its name from the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a> that forms its southern border, which, in turn, originated from the <a href="/wiki/Seneca_language" title="Seneca language">Seneca</a> word <i><span title="Seneca-language text"><i lang="see">ohiːyo'</i></span></i>, meaning "good river", "great river", or "large creek".<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The state was home to several ancient indigenous civilizations, with humans present as early as 10,000 BCE. It arose from the lands west of the <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Mountains" title="Appalachian Mountains">Appalachian Mountains</a> that were contested by various native tribes and European colonists from the 17th century through the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Indian_War" title="Northwest Indian War">Northwest Indian Wars</a> of the late 18th century. Ohio was partitioned from the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territory" title="Northwest Territory">Northwest Territory</a>, the first frontier of the new United States, becoming the 17th state admitted to the Union on March 1, 1803, and the first under the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Ordinance" title="Northwest Ordinance">Northwest Ordinance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MS_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MS-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WD_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WD-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> It was the first post-colonial <a href="/wiki/Slave_states_and_free_states" title="Slave states and free states">free state</a> admitted to the union and became one of the earliest and most influential industrial powerhouses during the 20th century. Although it has transitioned to a more <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_economy" title="Knowledge economy">information-</a> and <a href="/wiki/Service_economy" title="Service economy">service-based economy</a> in the 21st century, it remains an industrial state, ranking <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_GDP" title="List of U.S. states and territories by GDP">seventh in GDP</a> as of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ohio&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> with the third-largest manufacturing sector and second-largest automobile production.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Modeled on its federal counterpart, Ohio's government is composed of the executive branch, led by the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Ohio" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Governors of Ohio">governor</a>; the legislative branch, consisting of the bicameral <a href="/wiki/Ohio_General_Assembly" title="Ohio General Assembly">Ohio General Assembly</a>; and the judicial branch, led by the state <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Ohio" title="Supreme Court of Ohio">Supreme Court</a>. Ohio occupies 15 seats in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a>, the seventh-largest delegation.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Seven <a href="/wiki/Presidents_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Presidents of the United States">presidents of the United States</a> have <a href="/wiki/List_of_presidents_of_the_United_States_by_home_state" title="List of presidents of the United States by home state">come from Ohio</a>, earning it the moniker "the Mother of Presidents".<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Prehistory_of_Ohio" title="Prehistory of Ohio">Prehistory of Ohio</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Ohio" title="History of Ohio">History of Ohio</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Indigenous_settlement">Indigenous settlement</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sunwatch_Aerial_illustration_HRoe_2018_400px.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Sunwatch_Aerial_illustration_HRoe_2018_400px.jpg/220px-Sunwatch_Aerial_illustration_HRoe_2018_400px.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Sunwatch_Aerial_illustration_HRoe_2018_400px.jpg/330px-Sunwatch_Aerial_illustration_HRoe_2018_400px.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f9/Sunwatch_Aerial_illustration_HRoe_2018_400px.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="267" /></a><figcaption>Artist's conception of the <a href="/wiki/Fort_Ancient" title="Fort Ancient">Fort Ancient</a> <a href="/wiki/SunWatch_Indian_Village" title="SunWatch Indian Village">SunWatch Indian Village</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dayton,_Ohio" title="Dayton, Ohio">Dayton</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Archeological evidence of spear points of both the Folsom and Clovis types indicate that the Ohio Valley was inhabited by <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic people</a> as early as 13,000&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-knepper9_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knepper9-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> These early nomads disappeared from Ohio by 1,000&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-knepper9_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knepper9-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Between 1,000 and 800&#160;BC, the sedentary <a href="/wiki/Adena_culture" title="Adena culture">Adena culture</a> emerged. The Adena established "semi-permanent" villages because they domesticated plants, including sunflowers, and "grew squash and possibly corn"; with hunting and gathering, this cultivation supported more settled, complex villages.<sup id="cite_ref-knepper10_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knepper10-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The most notable remnant of the Adena culture is the <a href="/wiki/Great_Serpent_Mound" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Serpent Mound">Great Serpent Mound</a>, located in <a href="/wiki/Adams_County,_Ohio" title="Adams County, Ohio">Adams County, Ohio</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-knepper10_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knepper10-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 100&#160;BC, the Adena evolved into the <a href="/wiki/Hopewell_tradition" title="Hopewell tradition">Hopewell</a> people, who were also mound builders. Their complex, large and technologically sophisticated <a href="/wiki/Earthworks_(archaeology)" title="Earthworks (archaeology)">earthworks</a> can be found in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Marietta,_Ohio" title="Marietta, Ohio">Marietta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newark,_Ohio" title="Newark, Ohio">Newark</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Circleville,_Ohio" title="Circleville, Ohio">Circleville</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-knepper11_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knepper11-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> They were also a prolific trading society, their trading network spanning a third of the continent.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The Hopewell disappeared from the Ohio Valley about 600&#160;AD. The <a href="/wiki/Mississippian_culture" title="Mississippian culture">Mississippian culture</a> rose as the Hopewell culture declined. Many Siouan-speaking peoples from the plains and east coast claim them as ancestors and say they lived throughout the Ohio region until approximately the 13th century.<sup id="cite_ref-knepper13_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knepper13-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There were three other cultures contemporaneous with the Mississippians: the <a href="/wiki/Fort_Ancient" title="Fort Ancient">Fort Ancient</a> people, the <a href="/wiki/Whittlesey_culture" title="Whittlesey culture">Whittlesey Culture</a><sup id="cite_ref-knepper13_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knepper13-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Monongahela_Culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Monongahela Culture">Monongahela Culture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> All three disappeared in the 17th century. Their origins are unknown. The Shawnees may have absorbed the Fort Ancient people.<sup id="cite_ref-knepper13_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knepper13-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> It is also possible that the Monongahela held no land in Ohio during the Colonial Era. The Mississippian culture was close to and traded extensively with the Fort Ancient people.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:5NationsExpansion.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/5NationsExpansion.jpg/220px-5NationsExpansion.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="221" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/5NationsExpansion.jpg/330px-5NationsExpansion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/5NationsExpansion.jpg/440px-5NationsExpansion.jpg 2x" data-file-width="441" data-file-height="443" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Iroquois" title="Iroquois">Iroquois</a> conquests during the <a href="/wiki/Beaver_Wars" title="Beaver Wars">Beaver Wars</a> (mid-1600s), which largely depopulated the upper and mid-<a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a> valley.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Indians in the Ohio Valley were greatly affected by the aggressive tactics of the <a href="/wiki/Iroquois_Confederation" class="mw-redirect" title="Iroquois Confederation">Iroquois Confederation</a>, based in central and western New York.<sup id="cite_ref-knepper14_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knepper14-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Beaver_Wars" title="Beaver Wars">Beaver Wars</a> in the mid-17th century, the Iroquois claimed much of the Ohio country as hunting and, more importantly, beaver-trapping ground. After the devastation of epidemics and war in the mid-17th century, which largely emptied the Ohio country of Indigenous people<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (January 2019)">dubious</span></a>&#32;&#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Ohio#Dubious" title="Talk:Ohio">discuss</a></i>&#93;</sup> by the mid-to-late 17th century, the land gradually became repopulated by the mostly <a href="/wiki/Algonquian_languages" title="Algonquian languages">Algonquian</a>. Many of these Ohio-country nations were multiethnic (sometimes multi-linguistic) societies born out of the earlier devastation brought about by disease,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="no disease has been mentioned yet (January 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> war, and subsequent social instability. They subsisted on agriculture (corn, sunflowers, beans, etc.) supplemented by seasonal hunts. By the 18th century, they were part of a larger global economy brought about by European entry into the <a href="/wiki/Fur_trade" title="Fur trade">fur trade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-roseboom20_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-roseboom20-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the Indigenous nations that historically inhabited Ohio include the Iroquoian,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> the Algonquian,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> and the Siouan.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-knepper14-17_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knepper14-17-36">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ohio_country" class="mw-redirect" title="Ohio country">Ohio country</a> was also the site of Indian massacres, such as the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Creek_massacre" title="Yellow Creek massacre">Yellow Creek massacre</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gnadenhutten_massacre" title="Gnadenhutten massacre">Gnadenhutten massacre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-knepper43-44_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knepper43-44-37">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, when Natives suffered serious losses such as at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tippecanoe" title="Battle of Tippecanoe">Tippecanoe</a>, most Native tribes either left Ohio or had to live on only limited reservations. By 1842, all remaining Natives were forced out of the state.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Colonial_and_Revolutionary_eras">Colonial and Revolutionary eras</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/New_France" title="New France">New France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada_(New_France)" title="Canada (New France)">Canada (New France)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Country" title="Ohio Country">Ohio Country</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1763)" title="Treaty of Paris (1763)">Treaty of Paris (1763)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Quebec_(1763%E2%80%931791)" title="Province of Quebec (1763–1791)">Province of Quebec (1763–1791)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Reserve_(1763)" title="Indian Reserve (1763)">Indian Reserve (1763)</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_theater_of_the_American_Revolutionary_War" title="Western theater of the American Revolutionary War">Western theater of the American Revolutionary War</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">Treaty of Paris (1783)</a></div>
<p>During the 18th century, the <a href="/wiki/French_colonisation_of_the_Americas" class="mw-redirect" title="French colonisation of the Americas">French</a> set up a system of <a href="/wiki/Trading_post" title="Trading post">trading posts</a> to control the fur trade in the region. Beginning in 1754, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_France" title="Kingdom of France">Kingdom of France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a> fought in the <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a>, with various Native American tribes on each side. As a result of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1763)" title="Treaty of Paris (1763)">Treaty of Paris</a>, the French ceded control of Ohio and the remainder of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Northwest" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Northwest">Old Northwest</a> to Great Britain in 1763.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Before the American Revolution, Britain thinly exercised sovereignty over Ohio Country by lackadaisical garrisoning of the French forts.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;f&#93;</a></sup> Just beyond Ohio Country was the great <a href="/wiki/Miami_Tribe" class="mw-redirect" title="Miami Tribe">Miami</a> capital of <a href="/wiki/Kekionga" title="Kekionga">Kekionga</a>, which became the center of British trade and influence in Ohio Country and throughout the future <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territory" title="Northwest Territory">Northwest Territory</a>. By the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Proclamation_of_1763" title="Royal Proclamation of 1763">Royal Proclamation of 1763</a>, British lands west of <a href="/wiki/Appalachia" title="Appalachia">Appalachia</a> were forbidden to settlement by colonists.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Fort_Stanwix" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Fort Stanwix">Treaty of Fort Stanwix</a> in 1768 explicitly reserved lands north and west of the Ohio as Native lands.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> British military occupation in the region contributed to the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/Pontiac%27s_War" title="Pontiac&#39;s War">Pontiac's War</a> in 1763.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Ohio tribes participated in the war until an armed expedition in Ohio led by <a href="/wiki/Brigadier_General" class="mw-redirect" title="Brigadier General">Brigadier General</a> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Bouquet" title="Henry Bouquet">Henry Bouquet</a> brought about a truce. Another colonial military expedition into the Ohio Country in 1774 brought <a href="/wiki/Lord_Dunmore%27s_War" title="Lord Dunmore&#39;s War">Lord Dunmore's War</a>, kicked off by the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Creek_massacre" title="Yellow Creek massacre">Yellow Creek massacre</a> in Ohio, to a conclusion. In 1774, Britain passed the <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Act" title="Quebec Act">Quebec Act</a>, which formally annexed Ohio and other western lands to the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Quebec" class="mw-redirect" title="Province of Quebec">Province of Quebec</a> in order to provide a civil government and to centralize British administration of the <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>-based fur trade.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The prohibition of settlement west of the Appalachians remained, contributing to the American Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the start of the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>, the movement of Natives and Americans between the Ohio Country and <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen Colonies</a> had resulted in tension. <a href="/wiki/Fort_Pitt_(Pennsylvania)" title="Fort Pitt (Pennsylvania)">Fort Pitt</a> in Pennsylvania had become the main fort where expeditions into Ohio started. Intrusions into the area included General <a href="/wiki/Edward_Hand" title="Edward Hand">Edward Hand</a>'s 1778 movement of 500 Pennsylvania <a href="/wiki/Militia_(United_States)" title="Militia (United States)">militiamen</a> from Fort Pitt towards Mingo towns on the <a href="/wiki/Cuyahoga_River" title="Cuyahoga River">Cuyahoga River</a>, where the British stored military supplies which they distributed to Indian raiding parties;<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Colonel <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Brodhead" title="Daniel Brodhead">Daniel Brodhead</a>'s invasion in 1780 and <a href="/wiki/Brodhead%27s_Coshocton_expedition" title="Brodhead&#39;s Coshocton expedition">destruction of the Lenape Indian capital of Coshocton</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> a detachment of one hundred of <a href="/wiki/George_Rogers_Clark" title="George Rogers Clark">George Rogers Clark</a>'s troops that were <a href="/wiki/Lochry%27s_Defeat" title="Lochry&#39;s Defeat">ambushed near the Ohio River</a> by Indians led by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Brant" title="Joseph Brant">Joseph Brant</a> in the same year; a British and Native American attack on the U.S.' <a href="/wiki/Fort_Laurens" title="Fort Laurens">Fort Laurens</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> and the 1782 detainment and murder of 96 <a href="/wiki/Christian_Munsee" title="Christian Munsee">Moravian Lenape</a> pacifists by Pennsylvania militiamen in the <a href="/wiki/Gnadenhutten_massacre" title="Gnadenhutten massacre">Gnadenhutten massacre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The western theatre never had a decisive victor. In the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">Treaty of Paris</a> in 1783, Britain ceded all claims to Ohio Country to the new <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> after its victory in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Northwest_Territory">Northwest Territory</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Ordinance" title="Northwest Ordinance">Northwest Ordinance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territory" title="Northwest Territory">Northwest Territory</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ohio_Country_en.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Ohio_Country_en.png/220px-Ohio_Country_en.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Ohio_Country_en.png/330px-Ohio_Country_en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Ohio_Country_en.png/440px-Ohio_Country_en.png 2x" data-file-width="1217" data-file-height="920" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Country" title="Ohio Country">Ohio Country</a> indicating battle sites between American settlers and Indigenous tribes, 1775–1794</figcaption></figure>
<p>The United States created the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territory" title="Northwest Territory">Northwest Territory</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Ordinance" title="Northwest Ordinance">Northwest Ordinance</a> of 1787.<sup id="cite_ref-cayton3_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cayton3-53">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Slave_states_and_free_states" title="Slave states and free states">Slavery was not permitted</a> in the new territory. Settlement began with the founding of <a href="/wiki/Marietta,_Ohio" title="Marietta, Ohio">Marietta</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Company_of_Associates" title="Ohio Company of Associates">Ohio Company of Associates</a>, which had been formed by a group of American Revolutionary War veterans. Following the Ohio Company, the <a href="/wiki/Miami_Purchase" class="mw-redirect" title="Miami Purchase">Miami Company</a> (also referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/Symmes_Purchase" title="Symmes Purchase">Symmes Purchase</a>") claimed the southwestern section, and the <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Land_Company" title="Connecticut Land Company">Connecticut Land Company</a> surveyed and settled the <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Western_Reserve" title="Connecticut Western Reserve">Connecticut Western Reserve</a> in present-day <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Ohio" title="Northeast Ohio">Northeast Ohio</a>. Territorial surveyors from Fort Steuben began surveying an area of eastern Ohio called the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Ranges" title="Seven Ranges">Seven Ranges</a> at about the same time.
</p><p>The old Northwest Territory originally included areas previously known as <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Country" title="Ohio Country">Ohio Country</a> and <a href="/wiki/Illinois_Country" title="Illinois Country">Illinois Country</a>. As Ohio prepared for statehood, the <a href="/wiki/Indiana_Territory" title="Indiana Territory">Indiana Territory</a> was created, reducing the Northwest Territory to approximately the size of present-day Ohio plus the eastern half of the <a href="/wiki/Lower_Peninsula_of_Michigan" title="Lower Peninsula of Michigan">Lower Peninsula of Michigan</a> and the eastern tip of the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Peninsula_of_Michigan" title="Upper Peninsula of Michigan">Upper Peninsula</a> and a sliver of southeastern Indiana called "The Gore".
</p><p>The coalition of Native American tribes, known as the <a href="/wiki/Western_Confederacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Confederacy">Western Confederacy</a>, was forced to cede extensive territory, including much of present-day Ohio, in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Greenville" title="Treaty of Greenville">Treaty of Greenville</a> in 1795.
</p><p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Ordinance" title="Northwest Ordinance">Northwest Ordinance</a>, areas could be defined and admitted as states once their population reached 60,000. Although Ohio's population was only 45,000 in December 1801, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> determined that it was growing rapidly enough and accelerated the process via the <a href="/wiki/Enabling_Act_of_1802" title="Enabling Act of 1802">Enabling Act of 1802</a>. In regard to the <a href="/wiki/Leni_Lenape" class="mw-redirect" title="Leni Lenape">Leni Lenape</a> natives, Congress decided that 10,000 acres on the <a href="/wiki/Muskingum_River" title="Muskingum River">Muskingum River</a> in the present state of Ohio would "be set apart and the property thereof be vested in the <a href="/wiki/Moravian_Brethren" class="mw-redirect" title="Moravian Brethren">Moravian Brethren</a>&#160;... or a society of the said Brethren for civilizing the Indians and promoting Christianity".<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Rufus_Putnam,_the_&quot;Father_of_Ohio&quot;"><span id="Rufus_Putnam.2C_the_.22Father_of_Ohio.22"></span>Rufus Putnam, the "Father of Ohio"</h4></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:LandingOfThePioneers.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/LandingOfThePioneers.jpg/220px-LandingOfThePioneers.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/LandingOfThePioneers.jpg/330px-LandingOfThePioneers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/LandingOfThePioneers.jpg/440px-LandingOfThePioneers.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1747" data-file-height="1203" /></a><figcaption>The landing of <a href="/wiki/Rufus_Putnam" title="Rufus Putnam">Rufus Putnam</a> and the first settlers at <a href="/wiki/Marietta,_Ohio" title="Marietta, Ohio">Marietta, Ohio</a> in 1788.</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rufus_Putnam" title="Rufus Putnam">Rufus Putnam</a> served in important military capacities in both the <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>. He was one of the most highly respected men in the early years of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1776, Putnam created a method of building portable fortifications, which enabled the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a> to drive the British from Boston. <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> was so impressed that he made Putnam his chief engineer. After the war, Putnam and <a href="/wiki/Manasseh_Cutler" title="Manasseh Cutler">Manasseh Cutler</a> were instrumental in creating the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Ordinance" title="Northwest Ordinance">Northwest Ordinance</a>, which opened up the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territory" title="Northwest Territory">Northwest Territory</a> for settlement. This land was used to serve as compensation for what was owed to Revolutionary War veterans. Putnam organized and led the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Company_of_Associates" title="Ohio Company of Associates">Ohio Company of Associates</a>, who settled at <a href="/wiki/Marietta,_Ohio" title="Marietta, Ohio">Marietta, Ohio</a>, where they built a large fort, <a href="/wiki/Campus_Martius_(Ohio)" title="Campus Martius (Ohio)">Campus Martius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> He set substantial amounts of land aside for schools. In 1798, he created the plan for the construction of the Muskingum Academy (now <a href="/wiki/Marietta_College" title="Marietta College">Marietta College</a>). In 1780, the directors of the Ohio Company appointed him superintendent of all its affairs relating to the settlement north of the Ohio River. In 1796, President George Washington commissioned him as Surveyor-General of United States Lands. In 1788, he served as a judge in the Northwest Territory's first court. In 1802, he served in the convention to form a constitution for the State of Ohio.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Statehood_and_early_years">Statehood and early years</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Admission_to_the_Union" title="Admission to the Union">Admission to the Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_by_date_of_admission_to_the_Union" title="List of U.S. states by date of admission to the Union">List of U.S. states by date of admission to the Union</a></div>
<p>On February 19, 1803, U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> signed an act of Congress that approved Ohio's boundaries and constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> But Congress had not passed a formal resolution admitting Ohio as the 17th state. Although no formal resolution of admission was required, when the oversight was discovered in 1953, as Ohio began preparations for celebrating its sesquicentennial, Ohio congressman <a href="/wiki/George_H._Bender" title="George H. Bender">George H. Bender</a> introduced a bill in Congress to admit Ohio to the Union retroactive to March 1, 1803, the date on which the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_General_Assembly" title="Ohio General Assembly">Ohio General Assembly</a> first convened.<sup id="cite_ref-Blue_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blue-63">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> At a special session at the old state capital in <a href="/wiki/Chillicothe,_Ohio" title="Chillicothe, Ohio">Chillicothe</a>, the Ohio state legislature approved a new petition for statehood, which was delivered to <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, on horseback, and approved that August.<sup id="cite_ref-Blue_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blue-63">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ohio has had three capital cities: Chillicothe, <a href="/wiki/Zanesville,_Ohio" title="Zanesville, Ohio">Zanesville</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Columbus,_Ohio" title="Columbus, Ohio">Columbus</a>. Chillicothe was the capital from 1803 to 1810. The capital was then moved to Zanesville for two years as part of a state legislative compromise to get a bill passed. The capital was then moved back to Chillicothe from 1812 to 1816. Finally, the capital was moved to Columbus, to be near the state's geographic center.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_erie.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Battle_erie.jpg/220px-Battle_erie.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="137" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Battle_erie.jpg/330px-Battle_erie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Battle_erie.jpg/440px-Battle_erie.jpg 2x" data-file-width="610" data-file-height="379" /></a><figcaption><i><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lake_Erie" title="Battle of Lake Erie">Battle of Lake Erie</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Powell" title="William Henry Powell">William Henry Powell</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Although many Native Americans migrated west to evade American encroachment, others remained in the state, sometimes assimilating in part. Starting around 1809, the <a href="/wiki/Shawnee" title="Shawnee">Shawnee</a> pressed resistance to encroachment again. Under Chief <a href="/wiki/Tecumseh" title="Tecumseh">Tecumseh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tecumseh%27s_War" title="Tecumseh&#39;s War">Tecumseh's War</a> officially began in Ohio in 1811. When the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> began, the British decided to attack from <a href="/wiki/Upper_Canada" title="Upper Canada">Upper Canada</a> into Ohio and merge their forces with the Shawnee. This continued until Tecumseh was killed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Thames" title="Battle of the Thames">Battle of the Thames</a> in 1813. Most of the Shawnee, excluding the <a href="/wiki/Pekowi" title="Pekowi">Pekowi</a> in Southwest Ohio, were forcibly relocated west.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Ohio played a <a href="/wiki/Ohio_in_the_War_of_1812" title="Ohio in the War of 1812">key role</a> in the War of 1812, as it was on the front line in the Western theater and the scene of several notable battles both on land and in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Erie" title="Lake Erie">Lake Erie</a>. On September 10, 1813, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lake_Erie" title="Battle of Lake Erie">Battle of Lake Erie</a>, one of the major battles, took place near <a href="/wiki/Put-in-Bay,_Ohio" title="Put-in-Bay, Ohio">Put-in-Bay, Ohio</a>. The British eventually surrendered to <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Hazard_Perry" title="Oliver Hazard Perry">Oliver Hazard Perry</a>.
</p><p>Ultimately, after the U.S. government used the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Removal_Act_of_1830" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian Removal Act of 1830">Indian Removal Act of 1830</a> to force countless Native American tribes on the <a href="/wiki/Trail_of_Tears" title="Trail of Tears">Trail of Tears</a>, where all the southern states except for <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> were successfully emptied of Native peoples, the government panicked because most tribes did not want to be forced out of their own lands. Fearing further wars between Native tribes and American settlers, they pushed all remaining Native tribes in the East to migrate west against their will, including all remaining tribes in Ohio.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1835, Ohio fought with the <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Territory" title="Michigan Territory">Michigan Territory</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Toledo_War" title="Toledo War">Toledo War</a>, a mostly bloodless boundary war over the Toledo Strip. Only one person was injured in the conflict. Congress intervened, making <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>'s admittance as a state conditional on ending the conflict. In exchange for giving up its claim to the Toledo Strip, Michigan was given the western two-thirds of the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Peninsula_of_Michigan" title="Upper Peninsula of Michigan">Upper Peninsula</a>, in addition to the eastern third, which was already considered part of the territory.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Civil_War_and_industrialization">Civil War and industrialization</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Morganmap.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Morganmap.jpg/220px-Morganmap.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="224" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Morganmap.jpg/330px-Morganmap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c5/Morganmap.jpg 2x" data-file-width="433" data-file-height="441" /></a><figcaption>The route of <a href="/wiki/Morgan%27s_Raid" title="Morgan&#39;s Raid">Morgan's Raid</a> during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Ohio's central position and its population gave it an important place in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>. The Ohio River was a vital artery for troop and supply movements, as were Ohio's railroads. Ohio's industry made it one of the most important states in the Union during the war. It contributed more soldiers per capita than any other state in the Union. In 1862, the state's morale was badly shaken in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Shiloh" title="Battle of Shiloh">Battle of Shiloh</a>, a costly victory in which Ohio forces suffered 2,000 casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-knepper233-234_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knepper233-234-69">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Later that year, when <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_Army" title="Confederate States Army">Confederate</a> troops under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Stonewall_Jackson" title="Stonewall Jackson">Stonewall Jackson</a> threatened Washington, D.C., Ohio governor <a href="/wiki/David_Tod" title="David Tod">David Tod</a> recruited 5,000 volunteers to provide three months of service.<sup id="cite_ref-roseboom188_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-roseboom188-70">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> From July 13 to 26, 1863, towns along the Ohio River were attacked and ransacked in <a href="/wiki/Morgan%27s_Raid" title="Morgan&#39;s Raid">Morgan's Raid</a>, starting in <a href="/wiki/Harrison,_Ohio" title="Harrison, Ohio">Harrison</a> in the west and culminating in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Salineville" title="Battle of Salineville">Battle of Salineville</a> near <a href="/wiki/West_Point,_Columbiana_County,_Ohio" title="West Point, Columbiana County, Ohio">West Point</a> in the far east. While this raid was overall insignificant to the Confederacy, it aroused fear among people in Ohio and <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> as it was the furthest advancement of troops from the South in the war.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Almost 35,000 Ohioans died in the conflict, and 30,000 were physically wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-cayton129_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cayton129-72">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> By the end of the Civil War, the Union's top three generals—<a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Tecumseh_Sherman" title="William Tecumseh Sherman">William Tecumseh Sherman</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Philip_Sheridan" title="Philip Sheridan">Philip Sheridan</a>—were all from Ohio.<sup id="cite_ref-cayton128-129_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cayton128-129-73">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Standard_Oil.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Standard_Oil.jpg/220px-Standard_Oil.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="173" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Standard_Oil.jpg/330px-Standard_Oil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Standard_Oil.jpg/440px-Standard_Oil.jpg 2x" data-file-width="686" data-file-height="540" /></a><figcaption>The first <a href="/wiki/Standard_Oil" title="Standard Oil">Standard Oil</a> refinery was opened in Cleveland by businessman <a href="/wiki/John_D._Rockefeller" title="John D. Rockefeller">John D. Rockefeller</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>During much of the 19th century, industry was rapidly introduced to complement an existing agricultural economy. One of the first iron manufacturing plants, Hopewell Furnace, opened near <a href="/wiki/Youngstown,_Ohio" title="Youngstown, Ohio">Youngstown</a> in 1804. By the mid-19th century, 48 blast furnaces were operating in Ohio, most in the southern part of the state.<sup id="cite_ref-HOS_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOS-74">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Discovery of coal deposits aided the further development of Ohio's steel industry, and by 1853 Cleveland was the nation's third-largest iron and steel producer. The first <a href="/wiki/Bessemer_converter" class="mw-redirect" title="Bessemer converter">Bessemer converter</a> was purchased by the <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Rolling_Mill" title="Cleveland Rolling Mill">Cleveland Rolling Mill Company</a>, which became part of the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Steel_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Steel Corporation">U.S. Steel Corporation</a> after the merger of <a href="/wiki/Federal_Steel_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Steel Company">Federal Steel Company</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Steel" class="mw-redirect" title="Carnegie Steel">Carnegie Steel</a>, the first billion-dollar American corporation.<sup id="cite_ref-HOS_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOS-74">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The first open-hearth furnace used for steel production was constructed by the Otis Steel Company in Cleveland, and by 1892, Ohio was the second-largest steel-producing state, behind Pennsylvania.<sup id="cite_ref-HOS_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOS-74">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Republic_Steel" title="Republic Steel">Republic Steel</a> was founded in Youngstown in 1899 and was at one point the nation's third-largest producer. <a href="/wiki/Armco" class="mw-redirect" title="Armco">Armco</a>, now AK Steel, was founded in <a href="/wiki/Middletown,_Ohio" title="Middletown, Ohio">Middletown</a> in 1899.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry_in_Ohio" title="Petroleum industry in Ohio">Petroleum industry in Ohio</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="20th_century">20th century</h3></div>
<p>The state legislature officially adopted the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Ohio" title="Flag of Ohio">flag of Ohio</a> on May 9, 1902.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Dayton natives <a href="/wiki/Orville_and_Wilbur_Wright" class="mw-redirect" title="Orville and Wilbur Wright">Orville and Wilbur Wright</a> made four brief flights at <a href="/wiki/Kitty_Hawk,_North_Carolina" title="Kitty Hawk, North Carolina">Kitty Hawk, North Carolina</a>, on December 17, 1903, inventing the first successful airplane.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Ohio was hit by its greatest natural disaster in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Flood_of_1913" title="Great Flood of 1913">Great Flood of 1913</a>, resulting in at least 428 fatalities and hundreds of millions of dollars in property damage, particularly around the <a href="/wiki/Great_Miami_River" title="Great Miami River">Great Miami River</a> basin.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">National Football League</a> was originally founded in <a href="/wiki/Canton,_Ohio" title="Canton, Ohio">Canton, Ohio</a> in 1920 as the American Professional Football Conference.<sup id="cite_ref-profootballhof.com_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-profootballhof.com-78">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> It included <a href="/wiki/Ohio_League" title="Ohio League">Ohio League</a> teams in five Ohio cities (Akron, Canton, Cleveland, Columbus, and Dayton), none of which still exist. The first official game occurred on October 3, 1920, when the <a href="/wiki/Dayton_Triangles" title="Dayton Triangles">Dayton Triangles</a> beat the <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Panhandles" title="Columbus Panhandles">Columbus Panhandles</a> 14–0 in Dayton.<sup id="cite_ref-daytonlocal.com_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-daytonlocal.com-79">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Canton was enshrined as the home of the <a href="/wiki/Pro_Football_Hall_of_Fame" title="Pro Football Hall of Fame">Pro Football Hall of Fame</a> in 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-Akron-Canton_Football_Heritage_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Akron-Canton_Football_Heritage-80">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:A_scene_in_a_steel_mill,_Republic_Steel,_Youngstown,_Ohio.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/A_scene_in_a_steel_mill%2C_Republic_Steel%2C_Youngstown%2C_Ohio.jpg/220px-A_scene_in_a_steel_mill%2C_Republic_Steel%2C_Youngstown%2C_Ohio.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="281" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/A_scene_in_a_steel_mill%2C_Republic_Steel%2C_Youngstown%2C_Ohio.jpg/330px-A_scene_in_a_steel_mill%2C_Republic_Steel%2C_Youngstown%2C_Ohio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/A_scene_in_a_steel_mill%2C_Republic_Steel%2C_Youngstown%2C_Ohio.jpg/440px-A_scene_in_a_steel_mill%2C_Republic_Steel%2C_Youngstown%2C_Ohio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6379" data-file-height="8154" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">Iron</a> being converted to <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> for wartime efforts at Youngstown's <a href="/wiki/Republic_Steel" title="Republic Steel">Republic Steel</a> in 1941.</figcaption></figure>
<p>During the 1930s, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_States" title="Great Depression in the United States">Great Depression</a> struck the state hard. By 1933, more than 40% of factory workers and 67% of construction workers were unemployed in Ohio.<sup id="cite_ref-greatdepression_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greatdepression-81">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Approximately 50% of industrial workers in Cleveland and 80% in Toledo became unemployed, with the state unemployment rate reaching a high of 37.3%.<sup id="cite_ref-greatdepression_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greatdepression-81">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> American Jews watched the rise of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> with apprehension. Cleveland residents <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Siegel" title="Jerry Siegel">Jerry Siegel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joe_Shuster" title="Joe Shuster">Joe Shuster</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Superman" title="Superman">Superman</a> comic character in the spirit of the Jewish <a href="/wiki/Golem" title="Golem">golem</a>. Many of their comics portrayed Superman fighting and defeating the <a href="/wiki/Nazis" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazis">Nazis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Approximately 839,000 Ohioans served in the U.S. armed forces during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, of whom over 23,000 died or were missing in action.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Artists, writers, musicians and actors developed in the state throughout the 20th century and often moved to other cities that were larger centers for their work. They included <a href="/wiki/Zane_Grey" title="Zane Grey">Zane Grey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milton_Caniff" title="Milton Caniff">Milton Caniff</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Bellows" title="George Bellows">George Bellows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Art_Tatum" title="Art Tatum">Art Tatum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roy_Lichtenstein" title="Roy Lichtenstein">Roy Lichtenstein</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Roy_Rogers" title="Roy Rogers">Roy Rogers</a>. <a href="/wiki/Alan_Freed" title="Alan Freed">Alan Freed</a>, who emerged from the swing dance culture in Cleveland, hosted the first live rock 'n roll concert in Cleveland in 1952. Famous filmmakers include <a href="/wiki/Steven_Spielberg" title="Steven Spielberg">Steven Spielberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chris_Columbus_(filmmaker)" title="Chris Columbus (filmmaker)">Chris Columbus</a> and the original <a href="/wiki/Warner_Brothers" class="mw-redirect" title="Warner Brothers">Warner Brothers</a>, who set up their first movie theatre in Youngstown before the company relocated to California. The state produced many popular musicians, including <a href="/wiki/Dean_Martin" title="Dean Martin">Dean Martin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doris_Day" title="Doris Day">Doris Day</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_O%27Jays" title="The O&#39;Jays">The O'Jays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marilyn_Manson" title="Marilyn Manson">Marilyn Manson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dave_Grohl" title="Dave Grohl">Dave Grohl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Devo" title="Devo">Devo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macy_Gray" title="Macy Gray">Macy Gray</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Isley_Brothers" title="The Isley Brothers">The Isley Brothers</a>.
</p><p>Two Ohio <a href="/wiki/Astronauts" class="mw-redirect" title="Astronauts">astronauts</a> completed significant milestones in the <a href="/wiki/Space_race" class="mw-redirect" title="Space race">space race</a> in the 1960s: <a href="/wiki/John_Glenn" title="John Glenn">John Glenn</a> becoming the <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Atlas_6" title="Mercury-Atlas 6">first American to orbit the Earth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">Neil Armstrong</a> becoming the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11#Lunar_surface_operations" title="Apollo 11">first human to walk on the Moon</a>. In 1967, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Stokes" title="Carl Stokes">Carl Stokes</a> was <a href="/wiki/1967_Cleveland_mayoral_election" title="1967 Cleveland mayoral election">elected</a> mayor of Cleveland and became the first African American mayor of one of the nation's 10 most populous cities.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1970, an <a href="/wiki/Ohio_Army_National_Guard" title="Ohio Army National Guard">Ohio Army National Guard</a> unit <a href="/wiki/Kent_State_shootings" title="Kent State shootings">fired at students</a> during an antiwar protest at <a href="/wiki/Kent_State_University" title="Kent State University">Kent State University</a>, killing four and wounding nine. The Guard had been called onto campus after several protests in and around campus became violent, including a riot in downtown Kent and the burning of an <a href="/wiki/Reserve_Officers%27_Training_Corps" title="Reserve Officers&#39; Training Corps">ROTC</a> building. The main cause of the protests was the United States' <a href="/wiki/Cambodian_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Cambodian Campaign">invasion of Cambodia</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ohio was an important state in the developing ties between the <a href="/wiki/China%E2%80%93United_States_relations" title="China–United States relations">United States and the People's Republic of China</a> in the late 1970s and early 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-:05_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:05-87">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 59">&#58;&#8202;59&#8202;</span></sup>  Relations between the two countries normalized in 1979, during the second term of Ohio governor <a href="/wiki/Jim_Rhodes" title="Jim Rhodes">Jim Rhodes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:05_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:05-87">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 112">&#58;&#8202;112&#8202;</span></sup> Rhodes sought to encourage  economic ties, viewing China as a potential market for Ohio machinery exports.<sup id="cite_ref-:05_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:05-87">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 112">&#58;&#8202;112&#8202;</span></sup> In July 1979, Rhodes led a State of Ohio <a href="/wiki/Trade_mission" title="Trade mission">Trade Mission</a> to China.<sup id="cite_ref-:05_87-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:05-87">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 112">&#58;&#8202;112&#8202;</span></sup> The trip resulted in developing economic ties, a sister state-province relationship with <a href="/wiki/Hubei" title="Hubei">Hubei province</a>, long-running Chinese exhibitions at the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_State_Fair" title="Ohio State Fair">Ohio State Fair</a>, and major academic exchanges between Ohio State University and <a href="/wiki/Wuhan_University" title="Wuhan University">Wuhan University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:05_87-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:05-87">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 113">&#58;&#8202;113&#8202;</span></sup> Beginning in the 1980s, the state entered into international economic and resource cooperation treaties and organizations with other <a href="/wiki/Midwestern" class="mw-redirect" title="Midwestern">Midwestern</a> states, as well as <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Charter" title="Great Lakes Charter">Great Lakes Charter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Compact" title="Great Lakes Compact">Great Lakes Compact</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Great_Lakes_Governors" class="mw-redirect" title="Council of Great Lakes Governors">Council of Great Lakes Governors</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="21st_century">21st century</h3></div>
<p>Ohio's economy has undergone significant change in the 21st century, as the trend of <a href="/wiki/Deindustrialization" title="Deindustrialization">deindustrialization</a> has greatly impacted the <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">American Midwest</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rust_Belt" title="Rust Belt">Rust Belt</a>. Manufacturing in the Midwest experienced a stark decline during the early 21st century,<sup id="cite_ref-:0_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-88">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> a trend that greatly impacted Ohio. From 1990 to 2019, it lost over 300,000 manufacturing jobs, but added over 1,000,000 non-manufacturing jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-88">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Coinciding with this decline, Ohio has seen a large decline in union membership: 17.4% of Ohioan workers were union members in 2000, while 12.8% were union members in 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the wake of these economic changes, Ohio's state government has looked to promoting new industries to offset manufacturing losses, such as the production of <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">solar energy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle" title="Electric vehicle">electric vehicles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> One major program the state government launched was the "Third Frontier" program, created during the governorship of <a href="/wiki/Bob_Taft" title="Bob Taft">Bob Taft</a>, which aims to increase investment in Ohio and boost its technology sector.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> As of 2010, the Ohio Department of Development attributes the creation of 9,500 jobs to this program, with an average of salary of $65,000,<sup id="cite_ref-TF_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TF-92">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> while having a $6.6 billion economic impact with a <a href="/wiki/Return_on_investment" title="Return on investment">return on investment</a> of 9:1.<sup id="cite_ref-TF_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TF-92">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> In 2010 the state won the <a href="/wiki/International_Economic_Development_Council" title="International Economic Development Council">International Economic Development Council</a>'s <i>Excellence in Economic Development Award</i>, celebrated as a national model of success.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Many of the state's former industrial centers turned to new industries, including <a href="/wiki/Akron,_Ohio" title="Akron, Ohio">Akron</a> as a center for polymer and biomedical research, <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati" title="Cincinnati">Cincinnati</a> as the state's largest mercantile hub,<sup id="cite_ref-AE_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AE-93">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="The current source is insufficiently reliable (WP:NOTRS). (February 2023)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Columbus,_Ohio" title="Columbus, Ohio">Columbus</a> as a center for technological research and development, education, and insurance,<sup id="cite_ref-AE_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AE-93">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="The current source is insufficiently reliable (WP:NOTRS). (February 2023)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Cleveland,_Ohio" class="mw-redirect" title="Cleveland, Ohio">Cleveland</a> in regenerative medicine research and manufacturing, <a href="/wiki/Dayton,_Ohio" title="Dayton, Ohio">Dayton</a> as an aerospace and defense hub, and <a href="/wiki/Toledo,_Ohio" title="Toledo, Ohio">Toledo</a> as a national center for solar technology.<sup id="cite_ref-FC_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FC-94">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ohio's economy was also heavily afflicted by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">Great Recession</a>, as the state's <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment rate</a> rose from 5.6% in the first two months of 2008 up to a peak of 11.1% in December 2009 and January 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-96">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> It took until August 2014 for the unemployment rate to return to 5.6%.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-96">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> From December 2007 to September 2010, Ohio lost 376,500 jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> In 2009, Ohio had 89,053 foreclosures filings, a then-record for the state.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The median household income dropped 7% from 2006–07 to 2008–09, and the poverty rate ballooned to 13.5% by 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, Ohio <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> was $608.1 billion, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_by_Gross_State_Product_(GSP)" class="mw-redirect" title="List of U.S. states by Gross State Product (GSP)">seventh-largest economy among the 50 states</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LSC2016_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LSC2016-100">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, Ohio's total GDP accounted for 3.4% of U.S. GDP and 0.8% of world GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-LSC2016_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LSC2016-100">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Politically, Ohio has been long regarded as a <a href="/wiki/Swing_state" title="Swing state">swing state</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-:2_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-101">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> but the success of many <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> candidates in Ohio since the late 2000s has led many to question whether Ohio remains an electoral battleground.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-101">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On March 9, 2020, the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a> reached Ohio, with three cases reported.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-104">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> As of February 2023, over 41,600 Ohioans have died from COVID-19.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-104">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Ohio's economy was also heavily impacted by the pandemic, as the state saw large job losses in 2020, as well as large amounts of subsequent <a href="/wiki/Stimulus_(economics)" title="Stimulus (economics)">stimulus spending</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Geography">Geography</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Ohio_counties" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Ohio counties">List of Ohio counties</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Ohio" class="mw-redirect" title="List of cities in Ohio">List of cities in Ohio</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_villages_in_Ohio" class="mw-redirect" title="List of villages in Ohio">List of villages in Ohio</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Ohio_townships" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Ohio townships">List of Ohio townships</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ohio_public_lands" class="mw-redirect" title="Ohio public lands">Ohio public lands</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_lakes_in_Ohio" class="mw-redirect" title="List of lakes in Ohio">List of lakes in Ohio</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:PEVI-view-of-Put-in-Bay.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/PEVI-view-of-Put-in-Bay.jpg/220px-PEVI-view-of-Put-in-Bay.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/PEVI-view-of-Put-in-Bay.jpg/330px-PEVI-view-of-Put-in-Bay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/PEVI-view-of-Put-in-Bay.jpg/440px-PEVI-view-of-Put-in-Bay.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Put-in-Bay,_Ohio" title="Put-in-Bay, Ohio">Put-in-Bay</a> is located on <a href="/wiki/South_Bass_Island" title="South Bass Island">South Bass Island</a>, one of Ohio's <a href="/wiki/List_of_Lake_Erie_Islands" title="List of Lake Erie Islands">Lake Erie Islands</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Ohio's location has proven to be an asset for economic growth and expansion. Because it links the Northeast to the Midwest, much cargo and business traffic passes through its borders along its well-developed highways. Ohio has the nation's 10th-largest highway network and is within a one-day drive of 50% of North America's population and 70% of North America's manufacturing capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> To the north, Ohio has 312 miles (502&#160;km) of coastline with Lake Erie,<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> which allows for numerous cargo ports such as Cleveland and Toledo. Ohio's southern border is defined by the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a>. Ohio's neighbors are <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> to the east, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a> to the northwest, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Erie" title="Lake Erie">Lake Erie</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> to the west, <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a> on the south, and <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a> on the southeast. Ohio's borders were defined by <a href="/wiki/Metes_and_bounds" title="Metes and bounds">metes and bounds</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Enabling_Act_of_1802" title="Enabling Act of 1802">Enabling Act of 1802</a> as follows:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1211633275">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Bounded on the east by the Pennsylvania line, on the south by the Ohio River, to the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Miami_River" title="Great Miami River">Great Miami River</a>, on the west by the line drawn due north from the mouth of the Great Miami aforesaid, and on the north by an east and west line drawn through the southerly extreme of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan" title="Lake Michigan">Lake Michigan</a>, running east after intersecting the due north line aforesaid, from the mouth of the Great Miami until it shall intersect Lake Erie or the territorial line, and thence with the same through Lake Erie to the Pennsylvania line aforesaid.</p></blockquote>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ohioriver_bridge8475.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ohioriver_bridge8475.JPG/220px-Ohioriver_bridge8475.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ohioriver_bridge8475.JPG/330px-Ohioriver_bridge8475.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/07/Ohioriver_bridge8475.JPG 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="263" /></a><figcaption>View of the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a> near <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth,_Ohio" title="Portsmouth, Ohio">Portsmouth, Ohio</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Ohio is bounded by the Ohio River, but nearly all of the river belongs to Kentucky and West Virginia. In 1980, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Supreme Court">U.S. Supreme Court</a> held that, based on the wording of the cessation of territory by <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a> (which at the time included what is now Kentucky and West Virginia), the boundary between Ohio and Kentucky (and, by implication, West Virginia) is the northern low-water mark of the river as it existed in 1792.<sup id="cite_ref-Ohio_v_Kentucky_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ohio_v_Kentucky-109">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Ohio has only that portion of the river between the river's 1792 low-water mark and the present high-water mark. The border with Michigan has also changed, as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Toledo_War" title="Toledo War">Toledo War</a>, to angle slightly northeast to the north shore of the mouth of the Maumee River.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Geographic_regions_ohio.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Geographic_regions_ohio.svg/220px-Geographic_regions_ohio.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="294" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Geographic_regions_ohio.svg/330px-Geographic_regions_ohio.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Geographic_regions_ohio.svg/440px-Geographic_regions_ohio.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="870" /></a><figcaption>Geographic regions of Ohio</figcaption></figure>
<p>Much of Ohio features <a href="/wiki/Glacial_till_plains_(Ohio)" title="Glacial till plains (Ohio)">glaciated till plains</a>, with an exceptionally flat area in the northwest being known as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Black_Swamp" title="Great Black Swamp">Great Black Swamp</a>. This glaciated region in the northwest and central state is bordered to the east and southeast first by a belt known as the <a href="/wiki/Glaciated_Allegheny_Plateau" title="Glaciated Allegheny Plateau">glaciated Allegheny Plateau</a>, and then by another belt known as the <a href="/wiki/Unglaciated_Allegheny_Plateau" title="Unglaciated Allegheny Plateau">unglaciated Allegheny Plateau</a>. Most of Ohio is of low relief, but the unglaciated Allegheny Plateau <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Ohio" title="Appalachian Ohio">features rugged hills and forests</a>.
</p><p>Ohio's rugged southeastern quadrant, stretching in an outward bow-like arc along the Ohio River from the West <a href="/wiki/Northern_Panhandle_of_West_Virginia" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Panhandle of West Virginia">Virginia Panhandle</a> to the outskirts of Cincinnati, forms a distinct <a href="/wiki/Socioeconomics" title="Socioeconomics">socioeconomic</a> unit. Geologically similar to parts of West Virginia and southwestern Pennsylvania, this area's coal mining legacy, dependence on small pockets of old manufacturing establishments, and distinctive regional dialect set this section off from the rest of the state. In 1965, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Regional_Development_Act" title="Appalachian Regional Development Act">Appalachian Regional Development Act</a>, an attempt to "address the persistent poverty and growing economic despair of the Appalachian Region".<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> It defines 29 Ohio counties as part of Appalachia.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> While 1/3 of Ohio's land mass is part of the federally defined Appalachian region, only 12.8% of Ohioans live there (1.476&#160;million people.)<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Ohio_NA.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Map_of_Ohio_NA.png/220px-Map_of_Ohio_NA.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Map_of_Ohio_NA.png/330px-Map_of_Ohio_NA.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Map_of_Ohio_NA.png/440px-Map_of_Ohio_NA.png 2x" data-file-width="1967" data-file-height="1333" /></a><figcaption>Map of Ohio cities and rivers</figcaption></figure>
<p>Significant Ohio rivers include the <a href="/wiki/Cuyahoga_River" title="Cuyahoga River">Cuyahoga River</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_Miami_River" title="Great Miami River">Great Miami River</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maumee_River" title="Maumee River">Maumee River</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muskingum_River" title="Muskingum River">Muskingum River</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scioto_River" title="Scioto River">Scioto River</a>. The rivers in northern Ohio drain into the northern Atlantic Ocean via <a href="/wiki/Lake_Erie" title="Lake Erie">Lake Erie</a> and the <a href="/wiki/St._Lawrence_River" title="St. Lawrence River">St. Lawrence River</a>, and those in southern Ohio drain into the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi</a>. Ohio also includes <a href="/wiki/Bass_Islands" title="Bass Islands">Bass Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kelleys_Island,_Ohio" title="Kelleys Island, Ohio">Kelleys Island</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Law2015_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law2015-113">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Grand_Lake_St._Marys" class="mw-redirect" title="Grand Lake St. Marys">Grand Lake St. Marys</a> in the west-central part of the state was constructed as a supply of water for <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canals</a> in the canal-building era of 1820–1850. This body of water, over 20 square miles (52&#160;km<sup>2</sup>), was the largest artificial lake in the world when completed in 1845.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Category:Canals_in_Ohio" title="Category:Canals in Ohio">Ohio's canal-building projects</a> were not the economic fiasco that similar efforts were in other states.
